## Transcript

- [00:00] So youve probably seen a little kid react to a sudden bang or a terrifying clown or
- [00:04] some strange Santa at the mall, right?
- [00:07] Yeah, not pretty.
- [00:08] That child probably immediately reached for a parent, looking for comfort, and that same
- [00:12] kid might freak out if she was separated from her adult of choice, especially is she was
- [00:17] in an unfamiliar environment.
- [00:18] Its called attachment and if youve ever seen a clingy kid you know why its called
- [00:22] that.
- [00:23] They attach.
- [00:24] For a long time psychologists assumed that this was just an intense innate survival instinct;
- [00:28] I mean it makes sense that babies would be attached to their food source and bigger kids
- [00:32] would stick around to the people who helped them survive.
- [00:34] But in the 1950s American psychologists Harry and Margaret Harlow came along with a barrel
- [00:38] of monkeys, complicating and illuminating our idea of bonding with caregivers in what
- [00:43] has to be one of the saddest psychological experiments of all time.
- [00:47] The Harlows were breeding Rhesus macaque monkeys for their research on learning.
- [00:51] Part of the process involved separating babies from their mothers right after birth, which,
- [00:56] yeah, pretty cruel already.
- [00:57] They began noticing that baby monkeys were very attached to the blankets they had in
- [01:02] their cages, so they set up an experiment.
- [01:04] They created two artificial mothers.
- [01:07] One was a bare wire cylinder with a feeding bottle attached; they called that Wire Mother,
- [01:11] and the other was a cozier cloth and foam wrapped cylinder without a feeding bottle:
- [01:16] Cloth Mother.
- [01:17] It took no time at all to see the babys preference.
- [01:19] They overwhelming preferred the comfy cloth mama, clinging to it whenever they seemed
- [01:24] to be anxious or in need of comfort, and sometimes they fed from the Wire Mother with the baby
- [01:29] bottle while standing on the cloth one.
- [01:31] This discovery that attachment wasnt just about getting breakfast surprised a lot of
- [01:35] people.
- [01:36] It turns out that contact and touch are vital to attachment, learning, emotional well-being,
- [01:40] and psychological development.
- [01:42] As the brain and mind develop in infants so too do theyre emotions and social behavior.
- [01:47] Caregivers can greatly influence this development, and most psychologists will tell you that
- [01:51] how a child is raised early on can have a huge effect on how they view the world, other
- [01:55] people and themselves, not to mention how they react to stressful situations or sort
- [02:00] out moral dilemmas.
- [02:01] I mean, its a big complex challenging world out there and wire monkey baby mama just aint
- [02:07] gonna cut it.
- [02:11] [Intro]
- [02:14] Touch.
- [02:18] You can convey all sorts of emotions through touch.
- [02:20] A hug, a slap, a pat on the back, a poke on the side all convey meaning.
- [02:25] Babies learn a lot through touch.
- [02:26] Its how they feel security and trust.
- [02:29] You can imagine how all those poor macaques, who were separated not just from their mothers,
- [02:33] but everyone, grew up to have some social issues as adults.
- [02:37] That is maybe putting it too mildly.
- [02:39] Those monkeys showed many signs of being really disturbed, from trouble eating, to rocking
- [02:43] back and forth in a trance, to even engaging in self-mutilation.
- [02:45] Most of the macaques used in this study never recovered, and those who were forced into
- [02:50] pregnancy didn't know how to care for their own offspring.
- [02:53] Although the Harlow's research taught us a lot, it was inhumane and would never pass
- [02:57] today's ethical standards.
- [02:59] Monkeys, like humans, need to be loved.
- [03:02] And loving touch and care are tremendously important, but familiarity is also key to
- [03:07] attachment.
- [03:08] When you're little, a hug from a stranger is not the same as a hug from your mom or
- [03:10] your grandma or your dad or your zookeeper or whomever you're most comfortable and familiar
- [03:15] with.
- [03:16] The unfamiliar can cause anxiety.
- [03:17] And for some critters, all these factors need to come together sooner rather than later.
- [03:21] Some baby animals experience a critical period in early life when certain things have to
- [03:25] happen for normal development to occur.
- [03:27] For ducks and geese, that critical period occurs just after hatching when they accept
- [03:31] the first moving object they see as their mother.
- [03:33] This so-called "imprinting process" can be difficult to reverse, which can make things
- [03:37] a bit awkward if that moving thing is a golden retriever or a person or a beach ball.
- [03:42] Thankfully, human babies don't imprint.
- [03:44] The world would be a lot different and a lot more bizarre if they did.
- [03:48] Human babies do, however, feel a lot more comfortable around people, things, and settings
- [03:51] that they're familiar with.
- [03:53] They form emotional attachments, but not all attachments are created equal.
- [03:57] In the 1970's, American psychologist Mary Ainsworth created the "strange situation"
- [04:02] experiment to observe children's different attachment styles.
- [04:05] She'd put a one-year-old kid and their mom in an unfamiliar room, like a playroom at
- [04:09] the lab and then observe the child playing with the mother.
- [04:12] Eventually, the child would encounter something potentially stressful.
- [04:15] Like, a stranger would come in and interact with the child and then the mom would leave.
- [04:18] If the kid freaked out, the stranger would try to comfort them.
- [04:21] And then mom would come back and the stranger would leave.
- [04:24] Different children responded differently to the strange situation.
- [04:27] Ainsworth measured and observed 4 different categories of behavior, including separation
- [04:31] anxiety, the child's willingness to explore, stranger anxiety, and reunion behavior, or
- [04:36] how the child reacted when the mom returned, which was what she was particularly interested
- [04:40] in.
- [04:41] Ainsworth broke this behavior down into three main attachment styles: secure, insecure avoidant,
- [04:46] and insecure ambivalent.
- [04:47] About 70% of her subjects showed secure attachment and could happily explore their new digs and
- [04:52] interact with the stranger, so long as their mom was nearby.
- [04:55] If mom left, they might freak out a bit, but they greeted her return in a happy and positive
- [05:00] way.
- [05:01] About 15% of the kids demonstrated insecure ambivalent attachment.
- [05:04] They were afraid of the stranger, cried more and explored less, and had a major freak out
- [05:09] when mom left, only to act all salty and mad when she returned.
- [05:13] The last 15% or so showed insecure avoidant attachment.
- [05:17] They were fine with the stranger, kinda indifferent actually, didn't cling to mom, didn't seem
- [05:22] bothered when she left, showed little interest upon her return.
- [05:25] Ainsworth observed that sensitive, attentive mothers usually raised securely attached kids,
- [05:30] whereas less responsive mothers who often ignored their children, or super-anxious mothers
- [05:34] who obsessed over every little thing, often raised insecurely attached toddlers.
- [05:38] And then of course, on the extreme, the poor monkeys with their unresponsive, fake moms,
- [05:44] they became absolutely terrified in unfamiliar situations.
- [05:48] Attachment is vital.
- [05:49] It builds the foundation for our sense of basic trust and quite possibly for our adult
- [05:54] relationships, our motivation to achieve and our willingness to be bold, like that toddler
- [05:59] playing with new toys in a strange room.
- [06:01] Given what those messed-up monkeys taught us, it should be no surprise that disruptions
- [06:04] in attachment can bring a world of pain.
- [06:07] Babies raised under abuse or extreme neglect are often withdrawn and frightened, and many
- [06:11] parents who've engaged in abusive behavior were abused themselves as children.
- [06:15] Young kids exposed to extended abuse, trauma, and neglect are at higher risk for psychological
- [06:20] disorders, health problems, and substance abuse as adults.
- [06:23] Studies of children raised in understaffed Romanian orphanages, for example, found that
- [06:27] they scored lower in cognitive tests and were twice as likely to exhibit symptoms of anxiety
- [06:32] as their counterparts raised in quality foster homes.
- [06:35] It's certainly true that some kids can show remarkable resilience, but disrupted attachment
- [06:39] and care, often further complicated by social and economic marginalization of all kinds,
- [06:44] can leave life-long scars.
- [06:45] So if one of infancy's major social achievements is forming positive attachments, then one
- [06:50] of the biggest achievements in childhood would have to be achieving a positive sense of self.
- [06:55] This self-concept, or an understanding and evaluation of who we are, is usually pretty
- [06:59] solid by about the time we turn 12.
- [07:02] Charles Darwin proposed that our self-awareness begins when we can recognize ourselves in
- [07:06] a mirror.
- [07:07] This self-recognition typically doesn't occur in humans under 15 to 18 months.
- [07:10] And by the time that tot's heading to kindergarten, their self-concept is rapidly expanding.
- [07:15] They probably know their age, hair color, and family name.
- [07:18] Perhaps they know they're good at drawing and not so good at tree climbing, and they're
- [07:22] noticing the differences and similarities they share with other people.
- [07:25] Kids with positive self-images are more happy, confident, independent, and sociable.
- [07:29] So, how can we instill these values and security in kids?
- [07:34] And how does parenting affect development?
- [07:36] Whether your parents were aloof or affectionate, strict or lax, and whether they spanked you
- [07:40] or preferred to talk it out, one model of parenting would probably categorize them into
- [07:44] one of three major styles, all related to control.
- [07:48] The authoritarian parent makes rules with consequences and expects you to follow them
- [07:53] because "I said so!" and tends to not be very warm to their child.
- [07:57] Whereas the permissive parent often caves to their child's demands and exerts little
- [08:01] control over any of the child's behavior.
- [08:03] The authoritative parent, meanwhile, seeks to find a balance between the two.
- [08:07] They are demanding, but always explain the reasons for their rules, and are loving and
- [08:11] responsive.
- [08:12] And, of course, research indicates that finding that culturally appropriate sweet-spot between
- [08:15] too hard and too soft is the best way to go.
- [08:18] Now, in addition to that growing sense of self, two other important landmarks of childhood
- [08:22] and adolescence are the ability to discern right from wrong and the formation of individual
- [08:27] character.
- [08:28] When those two things combine, they give us morality.
- [08:31] Last week, we talked about Jean Piaget and his three-tiered model for cognitive development.
- [08:36] Well, American psychologist Lawrence Kohlberg modified and expanded on that, and blew it
- [08:41] up into his own three-level theory of moral development, which emphasized the notion that
- [08:46] our moral reasoning continues to develop throughout our lives.
- [08:49] Kohlberg outlined his theory by posing a series of moral dilemmas to children, teens, and
- [08:53] adults, and then analyzing their reasoning behind the judgments.
- [08:57] One of his most famous questions is known as the Heinz Dilemma.
- [09:00] A woman is dying of cancer; there's a special new drug that might save her, but the pharmacist
- [09:05] wants to make a big profit, so he charges a lot of money for it.
- [09:09] Her husband, Heinz, can't afford it and has tried everything, from fundraising to begging
- [09:14] the pharmacist, to no avail.
- [09:16] So, he steals the drug.
- [09:18] Was he wrong?
- [09:19] Kohlberg was less interested in people's answers than in the reasoning behind their choice.
- [09:23] He ended up organizing his subjects' responses into three basic levels of moral thinking.
- [09:28] Kohlberg found that if the subject was younger than nine, they were likely in what he called
- [09:31] the preconventional morality phase.
- [09:33] In this phase, kids are concerned with self-interest, but they're also starting to judge people
- [09:37] individually, based on their needs and point of view.
- [09:40] So, Heinz needed the medicine, and stealing it best served his needs.
- [09:44] But in the second phase, in early adolescence, our moral compasses seem to shift, during
- [09:49] what Kohlberg called the conventional morality phase.
- [09:52] Here, his subjects put an emphasis on conformity and worry about what would happen to Heinz
- [09:57] if he was seen as a criminal.
- [09:58] This phase seems to worry "what would people think?".
- [10:01] From adolescence on, Kohlberg believes, some people exist in the postconventional morality
- [10:06] phase.
- [10:07] This is a more complex adult morality, when we begin to account for differing values and
- [10:12] basic rights.
- [10:13] Laws are important, but some situations, like saving your beloved's life, might overrule
- [10:17] them.
- [10:18] This phase tops out with reasoning based on universal ethical principles and more abstract
- [10:23] reasoning.
- [10:24] Heinz was right to steal the medicine because people have a right to live.
- [10:27] Critics of Kohlberg's set up question his emphasis on moral thinking rather than moral
- [10:31] action, arguing that there's a big difference between reasoning out what you should do and
- [10:35] actually doing it.
- [10:36] But one thing's for sure, what we experience during our first years on this planet, the
- [10:40] nature and quality of our attachments, our sense of self, and our moral development,
- [10:45] they all set the stage of our adolescence and adulthood.
- [10:48] Today, your developing brain learned about Ainsworth's three styles of secure, insecure
- [10:53] avoidant, and insecure ambivalent attachment.
- [10:56] And about authoritarian, permissive, and authoritative parenting styles.
- [11:00] You also learned about developing self-concept and Kohlberg's stages of morality.
